Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Find $ P(Z>X+Y)$ where $X,Y,Z \sim U(0,1)$         up vote  2  down vote  favorite  1      I'm trying to follow a line in a derivation for $P(Z>X+Y)$ where $X,Y,Z$ are independent continuous random variables distributed uniformly on $(0,1)$.  I've already derived the pdf of $X+Y$ using the convolution theorem, but there's a line in the answer that says:  $P(Z>X+Y) = \mathbb{E}[\ P(Z>X+Y\ |\  X+Y )\  ]$ where $\mathbb{E}$ is the expectation.  I'm not familiar with this result. Could anyone give a pointer to a similar result if one exists?  Thanks.    probability  statistics     share | cite | improve this question      edited May 17 '12 at 20:16      user17762        asked May 17 '12 at 19:20       maliky0_o   330 1 2 10              add a comment |           6 Answers 6     active  oldest  votes            up vote  4  down vote      $$\mathbb{P}(Z>X+Y)=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)]=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)|X+Y]]=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{P}(Z>X+Y|X+Y)],$$
where second equality is the following property of conditional expectation :
$$\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{E}[X|Y]]=\mathbb{E}[X]$$
Intuitively, now that you know distribution of $X+Y$, you just need to "range"$^1$ through the values of $X+Y$, and find the probability of $Z>X+Y$ for each such value. This is exactly the expectation of the probability.  $^1$integrate against the density, i.e. $\int_0^2\mathbb{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv$     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 19:39             answered May 17 '12 at 19:31       Tom Artiom Fiodorov   768 4 20                  I was puzzled by the first equality, but is the principle just that  P(...) is some constant, and the expectation of a constant is a constant? If so that's a nice trick :) – maliky0_o  May 17 '12 at 19:44            No, I don't think you understand it properly. The first equality involves indicator function $\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)$, which is $1$ on the event $Z>X+Y$ and $0$ on the event $Z\leq X+Y$. Then expectation of such indicator is just the probability of the event $Z>X+Y$, by definition of expectation. – Tom Artiom Fiodorov  May 17 '12 at 19:48         add a comment |             up vote  3  down vote      This is not an answer to your question about the justification for the 
equation that is puzzling you, but I think the geometrical method
described below for solving the problem that may give you a different 
insight into the calculation of the desired probability $P\{Z > X+Y\}$.  The random point $(X,Y,Z)$ is uniformly distributed in the interior of the unit cube with diagonally opposite vertices $(0,0,0)$ and $(1,1,1)$.  The cube has unit volume and so the probability that $(X,Y,Z)$ is in some region is just the volume of that region.  Thus, $P\{Z > X+Y\}$ is the volume of the tetrahedron with vertices $(0,0,0)$, $(1,0,1)$, $(0,1,1)$ and $(0,0,1)$.  If we think of this
as an inverted pyramid whose base is the right triangle with vertices
$(1,0,1)$, $(0,1,1)$ and $(0,0,1)$ and apex $(0,0,0)$ is at 
altitude $1$ "above" the base,
then since the base has area $\frac{1}{2}$, we get
the volume as
$$P\{Z > X+Y\} = \frac{1}{3}\times (\text{area of base})\times(\text{altitude}) 
= \frac{1}{3}\times \frac{{3}}{2}\times1 = \frac{1}{6}.$$
Of course, if you have already computed the density of $X+Y$, then it is
straightforward to use the result given by Artiom Fiodorov to get
$$P\{Z > X+Y\}= \int_0^2{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv
= \int_0^1(1-v)\cdot v\;dv = 
\left.\frac{v^2}{2}-\frac{v^3}{3}\right|_0^1 = \frac{1}{6}.$$     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 22:00             answered May 17 '12 at 21:13       Dilip Sarwate   18.2k 1 25 64              add a comment |             up vote  2  down vote      Replacing the question in a larger context might help. Here is a result:   For every event $A$ in $(\Omega,\mathcal F,\mathbb P)$ and every sigma-algebra $\mathcal G\subseteq\mathcal F$, $\mathbb P(A)=\mathbb E(\mathbb P(A\mid \mathcal G))$.   To see this, recall that $U=\mathbb P(A\mid \mathcal G)$ is the unique (up to null events) random variable such that $\mathbb E(U;B)=\mathbb P(A\cap B)$ for every $B$ in $\mathcal G$. In particular, $B=\Omega$ yields $\mathbb E(U)=\mathbb P(A)$, as claimed above.  In your setting, $A=[Z\gt X+Y]$ and $\mathcal G$ is the sigma-algera generated by the random variable $X+Y$ hence $\mathbb P(\ \mid \mathcal G)=\mathbb P(\ \mid X+Y)$ by definition.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 24 '12 at 11:25       Did   239k 23 201 429                  This tries to address the question asked, which is to explain the identity written in the post, and not to compute $P(Z\gt X+Y)$ in the specific situation described in the post. – Did  Nov 24 '12 at 11:28        add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      A partial justification can be found in the Wikipedia entry on the Law of Total Probability .     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 20:06             answered May 17 '12 at 19:34       André Nicolas   442k 35 401 779              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      I think your question best understood using two discrete random variables. Suppose you have two random variables $X$ and $Y$ taking values $0,1,2,\ldots,\infty$. Now you are asked to compute the probability of the event $A = X > Y$.  So,
$$
\begin{eqnarray}
P(A) &=& P(X>Y)\\
\end{eqnarray}
$$
Here both $X$ and $Y$ are random. To compute this probability we need the notion of conditional probability. Here it is:
$$
P(A \cup B) = P(A|B) \times P(B)
$$  Now, come to the original problem. We first fix the value of any one random variable, say, $Y = y$. Clearly, $y$ is any value from $0,1,2,\ldots,\infty$, but $y$ can't take these values simultaneously. Now we compute $P(X > y|Y = y)$ and $P(Y = y)$.  Hence $P(X > Y)$ is nothing but $P(X > y|Y = y) \times P(Y = y)$. But we have probabilities of so many events like this for each and every possible value of $y$, again each of these events are mutually exclusive, because occurrence of any one, say, $y = 1$ prevents the occurrence of others i.e. $y = i, i \neq 1$. Therefore, to get the required probability, we need to sum up the probabilities for each of the m.e. events. Thus finally we get,
$$
P(X > Y) = \sum_{y = 0}^{\infty} \left[P(X > y| Y = y) \times P(Y = y)\right]
$$  If you are familiar with the basic definition of expectation of  random variable, then previous expression is actually,
$$
\begin{eqnarray}
P(X > Y) &=& \sum_{y = 0}^{\infty} \left[value \times \text{corresponding probability}\right]\\
P(X > Y) &=& E\left[P(X > y| Y = y)\right]
\end{eqnarray}
$$  Now, to make this result suitable for continuous variable, just replace the sum by integration w.r.t $y; (0 \leq y < \infty)$ and $P(Y = y)$ by $f_Y(y)$ i.e. density function of $Y$ at the point $y$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Jul 25 '15 at 22:05       Koushik   78 7              add a comment |             up vote  -1  down vote      I don't know if this helps since Dilip has given the answer, but the distribution of X+Y is triangular on [0,2] (isosceles with peak at X+Y = 1). So P(Z>X+Y) is the probability that a uniform on [0,1] is larger than  the triangular random variable on [0,2]. If X+Y>1 then Z cannot be >X+Y and the probability that X+Y is greater than 1 is 1/2. Now this is where taking the expectation fo the conditional probability helps in my proof.
P{Z>X+Y) =E[P(Z>X+Y|X+Y)]= ∫u P(Z>u|X+Y=u)du =∫u P(Z>u)du where u is integrated from 0 to 1.  The condition X+Y=u gets dropped because Z is independent of X+Y. P(Z>u)=1-u for 0<=u<=1.
hence P(Z>X+Y) =∫u(1-u)du  = 1/6. Just as Dilip showed.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered May 17 '12 at 22:40       Michael Chernick   3,965 1 11 21              1      Michael, could you elaborate on how this differs from what Artiom Fiodorov wrote, namely $\int_0^2\mathbb{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv$ – Dilip Sarwate  May 17 '12 at 23:20            Sure. Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Are $XZ$ and $YZ$ independent if $X$, $Y$ and $Z$ are all independent?         up vote  3  down vote  favorite       Let $X$ and $Y$ be independent random variables. Let $Z$ be a random variable such that $Z$ and $X$ are independent, $Z$ and $Y$ are independent. Are random variables $XZ$ and $YZ$ independent?    probability  probability-theory  independence     share | cite | improve this question     asked Apr 12 '16 at 20:15       Dello   122 2 10              add a comment |           4 Answers 4     active  oldest  votes            up vote  7  down vote  accepted      Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent random variables, both of which are equal to 1 with probability 1, and that $Z$ is independent of $X,Y$ and $Z=1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$, $Z=-1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$.  Then $\mathbb{P}(XZ=-1,YZ=1)=\mathbb{P}(Z=-1,Z=1)=0$, while $\mathbb{P}(XZ=-1)\mathbb{P}(YZ=1)=\frac{1}{4}$. So $XZ$ and $YZ$ are not independent.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:23       carmichael561   46k 5 40 77              add a comment |             up vote  2  down vote      Let $X$, $Y$, $Z$ be independent, with $X$ and $Y$ having nonzero mean.  Claim: $XZ$ and $YZ$ are independent if and only if $Z$ is constant.  Proof: If $XZ$ and $YZ$ are independent, then
$$E(XZ)E(YZ)=E(XZYZ).\tag1$$
Since the LHS of (1) equals
$E(X)E(Z)E(Y)E(Z)$,
while the RHS of (1) equals
$E(X)E(Y)E(Z^2)$,
we must have $\left( E(Z)\right)^2=E(Z^2)$.
The converse is immediate.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 12 '16 at 20:54             answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:35       grand_chat   17k 1 11 18                  ...If $E(X)\ne0\ne E(Y)$. – Did  Apr 12 '16 at 20:53            @Did Right! Editing to add that condition. – grand_chat  Apr 12 '16 at 20:54        add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      This example works also assuming $X$ and $Y$ non-constant.  Let $\Omega=\{1,2,...,16\}$ with uniform distribution.  $A_1=\{1,2,3,4\}$,
$A_2=\{1,5,6,7\}$,
$A_3=\{1,8,9,10\}$  then for any couple $A_i$, $A_j$ 
$$
P(A_i\cap A_j)=P(\{1\})=\frac 1 {16}=\frac 4 {16} \times \frac 4 {16} =P(A_i)P(A_j)
$$
so $Z=\chi_{A_1}$ ( indicator function of $A_1$) is independent on both $X=\chi_{A_2}$ and $Y=\chi_{A_3}$, and also $X$ and $Y$ are independent.  On the other hand $ZX=\chi_{A_1}\chi_{A_2}=\chi_{A_1\cap A_2}=\chi_{\{1\}}$ and $ZY=\chi_{A_1}\chi_{A_3}=\chi_{A_1\cap A_3}=\chi_{\{1\}}$, so they are the same random variable $\chi_{\{1\}}$ and since $P(\{1\})= \frac 1 {16}$ it cannot be self-independent.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 13 '16 at 7:09             answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:40       Marco Disce   1,348 11 16              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      Let $X,Y,Z$ be mutually independent Bernoulli variables with $E(X)=a\gt0,\ E(Y)=b\gt0,\ E(Z)=c\gt0.$ Then:
$$E(XZ\cdot YZ)=E(XYZ^2)=E(XYZ)=E(X)E(Y)E(Z)=abc.$$
$$E(XZ)E(YZ)=E(X)E(Z)E(Y)E(Z)=abc^2.$$
If $c\lt1$ then $E(XZ\cdot YZ)\gt E(XZ)E(YZ),$ whence $XZ$ and $YZ$ are not independent.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 12 '16 at 21:18             answered Apr 12 '16 at 21:00       bof   44.1k 3 47 109              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         If $E(X\mid Y)=Z$, $E(Y\mid Z)=X$ and $E(Z\mid X)=Y$, then $X=Y=Z$ almost surely         up vote  5  down vote  favorite  3       Let $X$, $Y$, and $Z$ be three random variables in $L^{1}(\Omega, \mathcal{F}, P)$. Suppose that we have $E(X|Y)=Z$, $E(Y|Z)=X$, and $E(Z|X)=Y$. Show that $X=Y=Z$ a.s.   I am able to show this in $L^{2}$; in fact, there, it's a very easy proof. I'm having trouble proving it for $L^{1}$, though, because in $L^{1}$, conditional expectations are defined in terms of sub $\sigma$-algebras of $\mathcal{F}$, and that's not the way they're expressed here. I suppose my main problem is expressing the conditional expectations given in the problem as ones I can use results for $L^{1}$ on. Can somebody please help me do that?    probability-theory  conditional-expectation     share | cite | improve this question      edited Nov 12 '15 at 15:04       Did   239k 23 201 429        asked Dec 13 '13 at 19:52       ALannister   1,598 3 10 39              add a comment |           1 Answer 1     active  oldest  votes            up vote  7  down vote  accepted      $E(X\mid Y) = Z$ implies that $Z$ is $\sigma(Y)$-measurable and in the same way $E(Z \mid X) = Y$ implies that $Y$ is $\sigma(X)$-measurable. By transitivity, this proves that $Z$ is actually $\sigma(X)$ measurable, so that $E(Z\mid X)=Z = Y$.  Et caetera.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Dec 13 '13 at 20:01       Siméon   9,494 1 15 47                  WOW! Thank you so very much!! There's not much more to show, right? B/c $X$ is certainly $\sigma(X)$-measurable... I like your avatar, by the way! – ALannister  Dec 13 '13 at 20:07             @GGG: Indeed. You can also finish with $X = E(Y\mid Z) = Y$ (because $Y=Z$). – Siméon  Dec 13 '13 at 20:09        add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Find $P(X<Y<Z)$ of exponential density functions         up vote  2  down vote  favorite       Let X, Y, Z be independent continuous random variables with exponential density functions  $\lambda e^{-\lambda x}$, $\mu e^{-\mu y }$ and $\nu e^{-\nu x}$ respectively, on $[0,\infty)$ (and zero otherwise)  Find $P(X<Y<Z)$  To be honest I dont not really know where to start with this question. I know that $\lambda e^{-\lambda x}$ is the poisson formula and i know the conditions for independence. For this reason i have not attempted the question as i have been completely clueless about it since i saw it.  Any help much appreciated. Many thanks    probability-theory     share | cite | improve this question     asked Nov 27 '13 at 21:19       Bernard.Mathews   396 3 20                  I fail to understand what prevented you to merely solve the integral. – Did  Nov 27 '13 at 21:40            This might mean you do not understand the concept of joint distribution (just a guess). – Did  Nov 27 '13 at 21:43        add a comment |           2 Answers 2     active  oldest  votes            up vote  2  down vote  accepted      Hint: What you want to find is
hat you need to calculate is
$$
P(X<Y<Z) = \iiint_{\{(x,y,z):x<y<z\}} f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z)\,dx\,dy\,dz
$$
Since $X,Y$, and $Z$ are independent, the joint density is the product of the individual densities: $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$. So, you really only need to calculate
$$
P(X<Y<Z) = \iiint_{\{(x,y,z):x<y<z\}} f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)\,dx\,dy\,dz
$$
 it remains for you to find the bounds on the integrals for the region $\{x<y<z\}$ in $\Bbb{R}^3$ remembering that the densities for $X, Y,$ and $Z$ are only non-zero when $0<x$, $0<y$, and $0<z$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 27 '13 at 21:36       Tom   8,699 9 16              add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      $$\lambda\mu\nu \int_0^\infty \int_x^\infty \int_y^\infty e^{-\lambda x -\mu y
-\nu z}\,dz\,dy\,dx $$     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 27 '13 at 21:39       ncmathsadist   40.5k 2 54 95              1      Considering that OP indicated to be in need of aid regarding the concept of joint probability distribution, this can at best be helpful as a comment. – Lord_Farin  Nov 27 '13 at 22:02        1      @Lord_Farin Solution posted before said comment. – Did  Nov 27 '13 at 22:44        add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Why is $P(X,Y|Z)=P(Y|X,Z)P(X|Z)$?         up vote  5  down vote  favorite       Could anyone derive or explain why the formula $P(X,Y|Z)=P(Y|X,Z)P(X|Z)$ is true? I understand conditional probability definition, but this formula confuses me and makes my head hurt x)  Here's another similar which I have struggled to understand:  $$p(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X})=p(\mathbf{x}|\mathbf{\theta},\mathcal{X})p(\mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X})$$  Could someone explain this one to me as well? This formula is from my neural networks book, but I have no idea why this is true, even though I understand the basic conditional probability formula $P(A|B) = \displaystyle\frac{P(A,B)}{P(B)}$. If I use this formula, what I would do for my book example is this:  $P(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X}) = \displaystyle\frac{P(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{\theta},\mathcal{X})}{P(\mathcal{X})}$  Could someone ease my frustration ;D  Thank you!    probability  bayesian     share | cite | improve this question      edited Aug 7 '17 at 6:42       Graham Kemp   73.6k 4 30 66        asked Feb 12 '13 at 14:11       jjepsuomi   3,330 8 30 54              add a comment |           2 Answers 2     active  oldest  votes            up vote  10  down vote  accepted      From
$$ P(X,Y|Z) = \frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(Z)}$$
$$ P(Y|X,Z) = \frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(X,Z)}$$
$$ P(X|Z) = \frac{P(X,Z)}{P(Z)}$$
we have
$$P(Y|X,Z) P(X|Z)=\frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(X,Z)}\frac{P(X,Z)}{P(Z)}=\frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(Z)}=P(X,Y|Z).$$
This is also intuitive: The probability that $X$ and $Y$ happen if we know that $Z$ happens is the same as the probability that $X$ happens when we know that $Z$ happens and that then $Y$ happens when we know that $X$ and $Z$ happen.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Feb 12 '13 at 14:19       Hagen von Eitzen   252k 20 251 457              add a comment |             up vote  5  down vote      Let's note $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}$ the probability $\mathbb P(...|\mathrm Z)$. Then apply your formula : $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X,Y) = \mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X | Y) \mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(Y)$.  Now $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X |Y) = \mathbb P_{\mathrm {Z, Y}}(X)$ because both are equal to $\frac{\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X,Y)}{\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(Y)} = \frac{\mathbb P(X,Y,Z)}{\mathbb P(Y,Z)}$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Feb 12 '13 at 14:25       Damien L   4,799 7 32              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . What is $P(X+Y+Z > 1)$?         up vote  1  down vote  favorite        Assume that $X,Y$ and $Z \sim U(0,1)$. Assume further $X,Y$ and $Z$ are mutually independent. What is $P(X+Y+Z>1)$?    I've written down the density function of $X,Y$ and $Z$. I've drawn a picture. I understand the range of a new variable $U=X+Y+Z$ should be between $0$ and $3$. But I'm stuck on how to find the distribution of $U$.    probability     share | cite | improve this question      edited Mar 12 '15 at 14:49      user147263        asked Mar 12 '15 at 14:40       Kian   124 7                  I've written down the density function of $X$, $Y$ and $Z$. I've drawn a picture. I understand the range of a new variable $U = X + Y + Z$ should be between 0 and 3. But I'm stuck on how to find the distribution of $U$. – Kian  Mar 12 '15 at 14:43            Oh, do you know $f_{X,Y,Z} = f_X f_Y f_Z$? – AlexR  Mar 12 '15 at 14:45        add a comment |           3 Answers 3     active  oldest  votes            up vote  0  down vote  accepted      $$P(X+Y+Z\leq 1)=\mu\{(x,y,z)\in[0,1]^3:0\leq x+y+z\leq 1\}=\frac{1}{6}$$
since the middle term is the volume of a simple pyramid.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 15:01       Jack D'Aurizio ♦   259k 29 246 595              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      The PDF of $U(0,1)$ is $f(x) = \chi_{[0,1]}(x)$. In other words, $P(X\le a) = a$ if $a\in[0,1]$ and $0$ else.
Now
$$P(X+Y+Z > 1) = 1 - P(X+Y+Z \le 1)$$
What do you know about the joint PDF of iid random variables?   We know $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$ so in this case we have
$$P(U \le a) = \int_0^a \int_0^{a-x} \int_0^{a-x-y} 1\cdot 1\cdot 1 \ \mathrm dz \ \mathrm dy \ \mathrm dx$$
Can you take it from here?     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 14:44       AlexR   22.4k 1 21 47                  I am struggling to see how you use $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$ to get $P(U \le a) = \int_0^a \int_0^{a-x} \int_0^{a-x-y} 1\cdot 1\cdot 1 \ \mathrm dz \ \mathrm dy \ \mathrm dx$ – Kian  Mar 12 '15 at 15:00            The area we integrate over is the set $$A:=\{(x,y,z)| x+y+z \le a\} \subset [0,1]^3$$ Thus $P(U\le a) = P(A) = \iiint_A f_{X,Y,Z}$ – AlexR  Mar 12 '15 at 15:04        add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      You don't actually need to explicitly find the distribution of $U$, or to do any calculus.  The integral that AlexR gave is just the volume of a certain three-dimensional figure (a subset of the unit cube) which you should be able to find by basic geometry.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 14:56       Michael Lugo   17.1k 3 32 73              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Find $ P(Z>X+Y)$ where $X,Y,Z \sim U(0,1)$         up vote  2  down vote  favorite  1      I'm trying to follow a line in a derivation for $P(Z>X+Y)$ where $X,Y,Z$ are independent continuous random variables distributed uniformly on $(0,1)$.  I've already derived the pdf of $X+Y$ using the convolution theorem, but there's a line in the answer that says:  $P(Z>X+Y) = \mathbb{E}[\ P(Z>X+Y\ |\  X+Y )\  ]$ where $\mathbb{E}$ is the expectation.  I'm not familiar with this result. Could anyone give a pointer to a similar result if one exists?  Thanks.    probability  statistics     share | cite | improve this question      edited May 17 '12 at 20:16      user17762        asked May 17 '12 at 19:20       maliky0_o   330 1 2 10              add a comment |           6 Answers 6     active  oldest  votes            up vote  4  down vote      $$\mathbb{P}(Z>X+Y)=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)]=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)|X+Y]]=\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{P}(Z>X+Y|X+Y)],$$
where second equality is the following property of conditional expectation :
$$\mathbb{E}[\mathbb{E}[X|Y]]=\mathbb{E}[X]$$
Intuitively, now that you know distribution of $X+Y$, you just need to "range"$^1$ through the values of $X+Y$, and find the probability of $Z>X+Y$ for each such value. This is exactly the expectation of the probability.  $^1$integrate against the density, i.e. $\int_0^2\mathbb{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv$     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 19:39             answered May 17 '12 at 19:31       Tom Artiom Fiodorov   768 4 20                  I was puzzled by the first equality, but is the principle just that  P(...) is some constant, and the expectation of a constant is a constant? If so that's a nice trick :) – maliky0_o  May 17 '12 at 19:44            No, I don't think you understand it properly. The first equality involves indicator function $\mathbb{1}(Z>X+Y)$, which is $1$ on the event $Z>X+Y$ and $0$ on the event $Z\leq X+Y$. Then expectation of such indicator is just the probability of the event $Z>X+Y$, by definition of expectation. – Tom Artiom Fiodorov  May 17 '12 at 19:48         add a comment |             up vote  3  down vote      This is not an answer to your question about the justification for the 
equation that is puzzling you, but I think the geometrical method
described below for solving the problem that may give you a different 
insight into the calculation of the desired probability $P\{Z > X+Y\}$.  The random point $(X,Y,Z)$ is uniformly distributed in the interior of the unit cube with diagonally opposite vertices $(0,0,0)$ and $(1,1,1)$.  The cube has unit volume and so the probability that $(X,Y,Z)$ is in some region is just the volume of that region.  Thus, $P\{Z > X+Y\}$ is the volume of the tetrahedron with vertices $(0,0,0)$, $(1,0,1)$, $(0,1,1)$ and $(0,0,1)$.  If we think of this
as an inverted pyramid whose base is the right triangle with vertices
$(1,0,1)$, $(0,1,1)$ and $(0,0,1)$ and apex $(0,0,0)$ is at 
altitude $1$ "above" the base,
then since the base has area $\frac{1}{2}$, we get
the volume as
$$P\{Z > X+Y\} = \frac{1}{3}\times (\text{area of base})\times(\text{altitude}) 
= \frac{1}{3}\times \frac{{3}}{2}\times1 = \frac{1}{6}.$$
Of course, if you have already computed the density of $X+Y$, then it is
straightforward to use the result given by Artiom Fiodorov to get
$$P\{Z > X+Y\}= \int_0^2{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv
= \int_0^1(1-v)\cdot v\;dv = 
\left.\frac{v^2}{2}-\frac{v^3}{3}\right|_0^1 = \frac{1}{6}.$$     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 22:00             answered May 17 '12 at 21:13       Dilip Sarwate   18.2k 1 25 64              add a comment |             up vote  2  down vote      Replacing the question in a larger context might help. Here is a result:   For every event $A$ in $(\Omega,\mathcal F,\mathbb P)$ and every sigma-algebra $\mathcal G\subseteq\mathcal F$, $\mathbb P(A)=\mathbb E(\mathbb P(A\mid \mathcal G))$.   To see this, recall that $U=\mathbb P(A\mid \mathcal G)$ is the unique (up to null events) random variable such that $\mathbb E(U;B)=\mathbb P(A\cap B)$ for every $B$ in $\mathcal G$. In particular, $B=\Omega$ yields $\mathbb E(U)=\mathbb P(A)$, as claimed above.  In your setting, $A=[Z\gt X+Y]$ and $\mathcal G$ is the sigma-algera generated by the random variable $X+Y$ hence $\mathbb P(\ \mid \mathcal G)=\mathbb P(\ \mid X+Y)$ by definition.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 24 '12 at 11:25       Did   239k 23 201 429                  This tries to address the question asked, which is to explain the identity written in the post, and not to compute $P(Z\gt X+Y)$ in the specific situation described in the post. – Did  Nov 24 '12 at 11:28        add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      A partial justification can be found in the Wikipedia entry on the Law of Total Probability .     share | cite | improve this answer      edited May 17 '12 at 20:06             answered May 17 '12 at 19:34       André Nicolas   442k 35 401 779              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      I think your question best understood using two discrete random variables. Suppose you have two random variables $X$ and $Y$ taking values $0,1,2,\ldots,\infty$. Now you are asked to compute the probability of the event $A = X > Y$.  So,
$$
\begin{eqnarray}
P(A) &=& P(X>Y)\\
\end{eqnarray}
$$
Here both $X$ and $Y$ are random. To compute this probability we need the notion of conditional probability. Here it is:
$$
P(A \cup B) = P(A|B) \times P(B)
$$  Now, come to the original problem. We first fix the value of any one random variable, say, $Y = y$. Clearly, $y$ is any value from $0,1,2,\ldots,\infty$, but $y$ can't take these values simultaneously. Now we compute $P(X > y|Y = y)$ and $P(Y = y)$.  Hence $P(X > Y)$ is nothing but $P(X > y|Y = y) \times P(Y = y)$. But we have probabilities of so many events like this for each and every possible value of $y$, again each of these events are mutually exclusive, because occurrence of any one, say, $y = 1$ prevents the occurrence of others i.e. $y = i, i \neq 1$. Therefore, to get the required probability, we need to sum up the probabilities for each of the m.e. events. Thus finally we get,
$$
P(X > Y) = \sum_{y = 0}^{\infty} \left[P(X > y| Y = y) \times P(Y = y)\right]
$$  If you are familiar with the basic definition of expectation of  random variable, then previous expression is actually,
$$
\begin{eqnarray}
P(X > Y) &=& \sum_{y = 0}^{\infty} \left[value \times \text{corresponding probability}\right]\\
P(X > Y) &=& E\left[P(X > y| Y = y)\right]
\end{eqnarray}
$$  Now, to make this result suitable for continuous variable, just replace the sum by integration w.r.t $y; (0 \leq y < \infty)$ and $P(Y = y)$ by $f_Y(y)$ i.e. density function of $Y$ at the point $y$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Jul 25 '15 at 22:05       Koushik   78 7              add a comment |             up vote  -1  down vote      I don't know if this helps since Dilip has given the answer, but the distribution of X+Y is triangular on [0,2] (isosceles with peak at X+Y = 1). So P(Z>X+Y) is the probability that a uniform on [0,1] is larger than  the triangular random variable on [0,2]. If X+Y>1 then Z cannot be >X+Y and the probability that X+Y is greater than 1 is 1/2. Now this is where taking the expectation fo the conditional probability helps in my proof.
P{Z>X+Y) =E[P(Z>X+Y|X+Y)]= ∫u P(Z>u|X+Y=u)du =∫u P(Z>u)du where u is integrated from 0 to 1.  The condition X+Y=u gets dropped because Z is independent of X+Y. P(Z>u)=1-u for 0<=u<=1.
hence P(Z>X+Y) =∫u(1-u)du  = 1/6. Just as Dilip showed.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered May 17 '12 at 22:40       Michael Chernick   3,965 1 11 21              1      Michael, could you elaborate on how this differs from what Artiom Fiodorov wrote, namely $\int_0^2\mathbb{P}(Z>v)f_{X+Y}(v)\;dv$ – Dilip Sarwate  May 17 '12 at 23:20            Sure.  He got it by integrating over the entire 3D space that he point out is a tetrahedron where the density is nonzero.  In my case I got to the last step explictly using the expectation of the conditional probability which shows how that rule gets used in the derivation. Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Are $XZ$ and $YZ$ independent if $X$, $Y$ and $Z$ are all independent?         up vote  3  down vote  favorite       Let $X$ and $Y$ be independent random variables. Let $Z$ be a random variable such that $Z$ and $X$ are independent, $Z$ and $Y$ are independent. Are random variables $XZ$ and $YZ$ independent?    probability  probability-theory  independence     share | cite | improve this question     asked Apr 12 '16 at 20:15       Dello   122 2 10              add a comment |           4 Answers 4     active  oldest  votes            up vote  7  down vote  accepted      Suppose that $X$ and $Y$ are independent random variables, both of which are equal to 1 with probability 1, and that $Z$ is independent of $X,Y$ and $Z=1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$, $Z=-1$ with probability $\frac{1}{2}$.  Then $\mathbb{P}(XZ=-1,YZ=1)=\mathbb{P}(Z=-1,Z=1)=0$, while $\mathbb{P}(XZ=-1)\mathbb{P}(YZ=1)=\frac{1}{4}$. So $XZ$ and $YZ$ are not independent.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:23       carmichael561   46k 5 40 77              add a comment |             up vote  2  down vote      Let $X$, $Y$, $Z$ be independent, with $X$ and $Y$ having nonzero mean.  Claim: $XZ$ and $YZ$ are independent if and only if $Z$ is constant.  Proof: If $XZ$ and $YZ$ are independent, then
$$E(XZ)E(YZ)=E(XZYZ).\tag1$$
Since the LHS of (1) equals
$E(X)E(Z)E(Y)E(Z)$,
while the RHS of (1) equals
$E(X)E(Y)E(Z^2)$,
we must have $\left( E(Z)\right)^2=E(Z^2)$.
The converse is immediate.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 12 '16 at 20:54             answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:35       grand_chat   17k 1 11 18                  ...If $E(X)\ne0\ne E(Y)$. – Did  Apr 12 '16 at 20:53            @Did Right! Editing to add that condition. – grand_chat  Apr 12 '16 at 20:54        add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      This example works also assuming $X$ and $Y$ non-constant.  Let $\Omega=\{1,2,...,16\}$ with uniform distribution.  $A_1=\{1,2,3,4\}$,
$A_2=\{1,5,6,7\}$,
$A_3=\{1,8,9,10\}$  then for any couple $A_i$, $A_j$ 
$$
P(A_i\cap A_j)=P(\{1\})=\frac 1 {16}=\frac 4 {16} \times \frac 4 {16} =P(A_i)P(A_j)
$$
so $Z=\chi_{A_1}$ ( indicator function of $A_1$) is independent on both $X=\chi_{A_2}$ and $Y=\chi_{A_3}$, and also $X$ and $Y$ are independent.  On the other hand $ZX=\chi_{A_1}\chi_{A_2}=\chi_{A_1\cap A_2}=\chi_{\{1\}}$ and $ZY=\chi_{A_1}\chi_{A_3}=\chi_{A_1\cap A_3}=\chi_{\{1\}}$, so they are the same random variable $\chi_{\{1\}}$ and since $P(\{1\})= \frac 1 {16}$ it cannot be self-independent.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 13 '16 at 7:09             answered Apr 12 '16 at 20:40       Marco Disce   1,348 11 16              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      Let $X,Y,Z$ be mutually independent Bernoulli variables with $E(X)=a\gt0,\ E(Y)=b\gt0,\ E(Z)=c\gt0.$ Then:
$$E(XZ\cdot YZ)=E(XYZ^2)=E(XYZ)=E(X)E(Y)E(Z)=abc.$$
$$E(XZ)E(YZ)=E(X)E(Z)E(Y)E(Z)=abc^2.$$
If $c\lt1$ then $E(XZ\cdot YZ)\gt E(XZ)E(YZ),$ whence $XZ$ and $YZ$ are not independent.     share | cite | improve this answer      edited Apr 12 '16 at 21:18             answered Apr 12 '16 at 21:00       bof   44.1k 3 47 109              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Suppose that we have $E(X|Y)=Z$, $E(Y|Z)=X$, and $E(Z|X)=Y$. Show that $X=Y=Z$ a.s.   I am able to show this in $L^{2}$; in fact, there, it's a very easy proof. I'm having trouble proving it for $L^{1}$, though, because in $L^{1}$, conditional expectations are defined in terms of sub $\sigma$-algebras of $\mathcal{F}$, and that's not the way they're expressed here. I suppose my main problem is expressing the conditional expectations given in the problem as ones I can use results for $L^{1}$ on. Can somebody please help me do that?    probability-theory  conditional-expectation     share | cite | improve this question      edited Nov 12 '15 at 15:04       Did   239k 23 201 429        asked Dec 13 '13 at 19:52       ALannister   1,598 3 10 39              add a comment |           1 Answer 1     active  oldest  votes            up vote  7  down vote  accepted      $E(X\mid Y) = Z$ implies that $Z$ is $\sigma(Y)$-measurable and in the same way $E(Z \mid X) = Y$ implies that $Y$ is $\sigma(X)$-measurable. By transitivity, this proves that $Z$ is actually $\sigma(X)$ measurable, so that $E(Z\mid X)=Z = Y$.  Et caetera.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Dec 13 '13 at 20:01       Siméon   9,494 1 15 47                  WOW! Thank you so very much!! There's not much more to show, right? B/c $X$ is certainly $\sigma(X)$-measurable... I like your avatar, by the way! – ALannister  Dec 13 '13 at 20:07             @GGG: Indeed. You can also finish with $X = E(Y\mid Z) = Y$ (because $Y=Z$). – Siméon  Dec 13 '13 at 20:09        add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . hat you need to calculate is
$$
P(X<Y<Z) = \iiint_{\{(x,y,z):x<y<z\}} f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z)\,dx\,dy\,dz
$$
Since $X,Y$, and $Z$ are independent, the joint density is the product of the individual densities: $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$. So, you really only need to calculate
$$
P(X<Y<Z) = \iiint_{\{(x,y,z):x<y<z\}} f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)\,dx\,dy\,dz
$$
 it remains for you to find the bounds on the integrals for the region $\{x<y<z\}$ in $\Bbb{R}^3$ remembering that the densities for $X, Y,$ and $Z$ are only non-zero when $0<x$, $0<y$, and $0<z$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 27 '13 at 21:36       Tom   8,699 9 16              add a comment |             up vote  1  down vote      $$\lambda\mu\nu \int_0^\infty \int_x^\infty \int_y^\infty e^{-\lambda x -\mu y
-\nu z}\,dz\,dy\,dx $$     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Nov 27 '13 at 21:39       ncmathsadist   40.5k 2 54 95              1      Considering that OP indicated to be in need of aid regarding the concept of joint probability distribution, this can at best be helpful as a comment. – Lord_Farin  Nov 27 '13 at 22:02        1      @Lord_Farin Solution posted before said comment. – Did  Nov 27 '13 at 22:44        add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         Why is $P(X,Y|Z)=P(Y|X,Z)P(X|Z)$?         up vote  5  down vote  favorite       Could anyone derive or explain why the formula $P(X,Y|Z)=P(Y|X,Z)P(X|Z)$ is true? I understand conditional probability definition, but this formula confuses me and makes my head hurt x)  Here's another similar which I have struggled to understand:  $$p(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X})=p(\mathbf{x}|\mathbf{\theta},\mathcal{X})p(\mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X})$$  Could someone explain this one to me as well? This formula is from my neural networks book, but I have no idea why this is true, even though I understand the basic conditional probability formula $P(A|B) = \displaystyle\frac{P(A,B)}{P(B)}$. If I use this formula, what I would do for my book example is this:  $P(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{\theta}|\mathcal{X}) = \displaystyle\frac{P(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{\theta},\mathcal{X})}{P(\mathcal{X})}$  Could someone ease my frustration ;D  Thank you!    probability  bayesian     share | cite | improve this question      edited Aug 7 '17 at 6:42       Graham Kemp   73.6k 4 30 66        asked Feb 12 '13 at 14:11       jjepsuomi   3,330 8 30 54              add a comment |           2 Answers 2     active  oldest  votes            up vote  10  down vote  accepted      From
$$ P(X,Y|Z) = \frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(Z)}$$
$$ P(Y|X,Z) = \frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(X,Z)}$$
$$ P(X|Z) = \frac{P(X,Z)}{P(Z)}$$
we have
$$P(Y|X,Z) P(X|Z)=\frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(X,Z)}\frac{P(X,Z)}{P(Z)}=\frac{P(X,Y,Z)}{P(Z)}=P(X,Y|Z).$$
This is also intuitive: The probability that $X$ and $Y$ happen if we know that $Z$ happens is the same as the probability that $X$ happens when we know that $Z$ happens and that then $Y$ happens when we know that $X$ and $Z$ happen.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Feb 12 '13 at 14:19       Hagen von Eitzen   252k 20 251 457              add a comment |             up vote  5  down vote      Let's note $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}$ the probability $\mathbb P(...|\mathrm Z)$. Then apply your formula : $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X,Y) = \mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X | Y) \mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(Y)$.  Now $\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X |Y) = \mathbb P_{\mathrm {Z, Y}}(X)$ because both are equal to $\frac{\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(X,Y)}{\mathbb P_{\mathrm Z}(Y)} = \frac{\mathbb P(X,Y,Z)}{\mathbb P(Y,Z)}$.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Feb 12 '13 at 14:25       Damien L   4,799 7 32              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service . Join them; it only takes a minute:   Sign up    Here's how it works:   Anybody can ask a question  Anybody can answer  The best answers are voted up and rise to the top         $X,Y,Z$ are i.i.d $U(0,1)$. What is $P(X+Y+Z > 1)$?         up vote  1  down vote  favorite        Assume that $X,Y$ and $Z \sim U(0,1)$. Assume further $X,Y$ and $Z$ are mutually independent. What is $P(X+Y+Z>1)$?    I've written down the density function of $X,Y$ and $Z$. I've drawn a picture. I understand the range of a new variable $U=X+Y+Z$ should be between $0$ and $3$. But I'm stuck on how to find the distribution of $U$.    probability     share | cite | improve this question      edited Mar 12 '15 at 14:49      user147263        asked Mar 12 '15 at 14:40       Kian   124 7                  I've written down the density function of $X$, $Y$ and $Z$. I've drawn a picture. I understand the range of a new variable $U = X + Y + Z$ should be between 0 and 3. But I'm stuck on how to find the distribution of $U$. – Kian  Mar 12 '15 at 14:43            Oh, do you know $f_{X,Y,Z} = f_X f_Y f_Z$? – AlexR  Mar 12 '15 at 14:45        add a comment |           3 Answers 3     active  oldest  votes            up vote  0  down vote  accepted      $$P(X+Y+Z\leq 1)=\mu\{(x,y,z)\in[0,1]^3:0\leq x+y+z\leq 1\}=\frac{1}{6}$$
since the middle term is the volume of a simple pyramid.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 15:01       Jack D'Aurizio ♦   259k 29 246 595              add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      The PDF of $U(0,1)$ is $f(x) = \chi_{[0,1]}(x)$. In other words, $P(X\le a) = a$ if $a\in[0,1]$ and $0$ else.
Now
$$P(X+Y+Z > 1) = 1 - P(X+Y+Z \le 1)$$
What do you know about the joint PDF of iid random variables?   We know $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$ so in this case we have
$$P(U \le a) = \int_0^a \int_0^{a-x} \int_0^{a-x-y} 1\cdot 1\cdot 1 \ \mathrm dz \ \mathrm dy \ \mathrm dx$$
Can you take it from here?     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 14:44       AlexR   22.4k 1 21 47                  I am struggling to see how you use $f_{X,Y,Z}(x,y,z) = f_X(x) f_Y(y) f_Z(z)$ to get $P(U \le a) = \int_0^a \int_0^{a-x} \int_0^{a-x-y} 1\cdot 1\cdot 1 \ \mathrm dz \ \mathrm dy \ \mathrm dx$ – Kian  Mar 12 '15 at 15:00            The area we integrate over is the set $$A:=\{(x,y,z)| x+y+z \le a\} \subset [0,1]^3$$ Thus $P(U\le a) = P(A) = \iiint_A f_{X,Y,Z}$ – AlexR  Mar 12 '15 at 15:04        add a comment |             up vote  0  down vote      You don't actually need to explicitly find the distribution of $U$, or to do any calculus.  The integral that AlexR gave is just the volume of a certain three-dimensional figure (a subset of the unit cube) which you should be able to find by basic geometry.     share | cite | improve this answer     answered Mar 12 '15 at 14:56       Michael Lugo   17.1k 3 32 73              add a comment |            Your Answer             draft saved  draft discarded             Sign up or log in    Sign up using Google   Sign up using Facebook   Sign up using Email and Password        Post as a guest       Name     Email             Post as a guest       Name     Email            discard  By posting your answer, you agree to the privacy policy and terms of service .
